<div id="review_2076593372" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2076593372" />
    <a title="Barry Pierce" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4593541-barry-pierce"><img alt="Barry Pierce" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1588975731p2/4593541.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2076593372?book_show_action=true">Aug 01, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Barry Pierce" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Barry Pierce" href="/user/show/4593541-barry-pierce">Barry Pierce</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/30308970-swing-time">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4593541-barry-pierce?shelf=21st-century">21st-century</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4593541-barry-pierce?shelf=read-in-2017">read-in-2017</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2076593372" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15891876096133055576"><i>'Swing! Dig the rhythm! Swing! Dig the message!'</i> - 'Swing' from Bernstein's Wonderful Town<br/><br/>Possibly Zadie Smith's most divisive novel to date, <i>Swing Time</i> is a tale of two brown girls, both dancers, dreaming of being the Ginger to someone's Fred. Both girls, Tracey and our unnamed narrator, grow up on the estates of North London (or, as Smith herself puts it, a North London of the mind). Our narrator lives with her black activist mother, she is something of a mix between Assata Shakur and Diane Ab</span>
  <span id="freeText15891876096133055576" style="display:none"><i>'Swing! Dig the rhythm! Swing! Dig the message!'</i> - 'Swing' from Bernstein's Wonderful Town<br /><br />Possibly Zadie Smith's most divisive novel to date, <i>Swing Time</i> is a tale of two brown girls, both dancers, dreaming of being the Ginger to someone's Fred. Both girls, Tracey and our unnamed narrator, grow up on the estates of North London (or, as Smith herself puts it, a North London of the mind). Our narrator lives with her black activist mother, she is something of a mix between Assata Shakur and Diane Abbott, whilst Tracey lives with her white Croydon facelift'd mother who seems to be a somewhat more mature Vicky Pollard. As with most Smith novels, class divides all. The novel follows the two girls and our narrator's eventual career in the media as an assistant to Madonna standee, Aimee.<br /><br />It's a road well-worn. A novel about two girls from different class backgrounds magically kindling a friendship. Just off the top of my head I can recall numerous other authors who've written books on the exact same subject; Maeve Binchy, Edna O'Brien, Toni Morrison, Elena Ferrante. However, with Smith, it didn't feel like I've been here before. The sections of the novel dealing with our narrator's relationship with Tracey are by far its saving grace. Never a chore to read (unlike other sections) everything is so naturalistic between them and Smith writes this relationship with such intimate detail that at times one forgets that this novel is fiction. The girls' love of the golden age of musicals obviously aided in my adoration of these sections, with numerous references to <i>Gypsy</i>, <i>Show Boat</i>, and <i>Guys and Dolls</i>, I felt right at home laughing at every joke and understanding every reference.<br /><br />However, this novel is not without flaws. The largest flaws for me were the West Africa sections. In the novel, the superstar Aimee (to whom our narrator is a PA) funds the building of a school in a West African nation and thus our narrator must make numerous trips there to make sure that everything is going smoothly. Whilst these sections did serve as foils for our narrator's naivety, they are also the sections in which a whole lot of nothing happens. Often slid in between sections detailing the narrator's friendship with Tracey, these sections eventually became utterly labourious. I must admit at times skimming a couple of these sections in order to get back to Tracey and her life. <br /><br />Another flaw of the novel is its sheer scale. There is absolutely no reason for this book to be the behemoth it is. Honestly, you want to save the trees? I've a solution. Hire better editors. This novel could have been cleaned up a lot more and could've lost maybe 150 pages. If Orwell managed to do <i>Animal Farm</i> in 95 pages then you have no excuse Zadie. <br /><br />This novel had an interesting time with the press. The Guardian called it <a href="https://www.theguardian.com/books/2016/nov/13/swing-time-zadie-smith-review" rel="nofollow noopener">'her finest novel'</a> whilst The Irish Times said it is <a href="https://www.irishtimes.com/culture/books/swing-time-review-zadie-smith-s-new-novel-can-t-overcome-faults-1.2858001" rel="nofollow noopener">'lacking a consistent narrative drive, an interesting voice or a compelling point of view'</a>. I think I fall somewhere in the middle of these reviews. Is it Smith's best? God no. Is it a good starting point with Smith? Dear god no. <i>Swing Time</i> is a minor novel in Smith's oeuvre. I'm starting to feel we'll never reach the heights of <i>White Teeth</i> ever again. I would align this novel more with <i>The Autograph Man</i>, another Smith novel which had a hard time with the public but which I found to be throughly enjoyable. And just like <i>The Autograph Man</i>, I think <i>Swing Time</i> is more a Smith novel for Smith fans. A notch on the bedpost for completionists. Because for every <i>Middlemarch</i>, there has to be a <i>Silas Marner</i>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15891876096133055576" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1562281679" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1562281679" />
    <a title="Jenna" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/38727943-jenna"><img alt="Jenna" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1451400366p2/38727943.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1562281679?book_show_action=true">Nov 30, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jenna" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jenna" href="/user/show/38727943-jenna">Jenna</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/38727943-jenna?shelf=2016-reading-challenge">2016-reading-challenge</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/38727943-jenna?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1562281679" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer59526067599381957">Oh, man. What a letdown. Awarding a Zadie book a lackluster rating elicits a very uncomfortable and unfamiliar feeling from me, sort of like going to the polls and finding you've just accidentally cast your vote for the opposite candidate than intended. (Not that I expect any voting or poll-related similes to have any great resonance in fall 2016 USA.... Also, that was just metaphorical speaking; you can bet I triple-checked my vote for accuracy before submitting it this month!...which already f</span>
  <span id="freeText59526067599381957" style="display:none">Oh, man. What a letdown. Awarding a Zadie book a lackluster rating elicits a very uncomfortable and unfamiliar feeling from me, sort of like going to the polls and finding you've just accidentally cast your vote for the opposite candidate than intended. (Not that I expect any voting or poll-related similes to have any great resonance in fall 2016 USA.... Also, that was just metaphorical speaking; you can bet I triple-checked my vote for accuracy before submitting it this month!...which already feels a year long...but I digress.)<br /><br /><br />I'm disappointed with myself for not loving this book given my general love for Zadie Smith and her other works. NW is one of my favorite novels of all time, and one that captured better than anything I've read my own specific experience in growing up navigating the "blocks" of poor/working class community housing. It's such an amazing book in my view that I can't even bring myself to describe my appreciation: it left me feeling that I wasn't fucking worthy of even attempting to articulate her brilliance. Maybe on my deathbed I'll try to review it, was my ultimate conclusion, when I can hopefully channel the spirit of Virginia Woolf to express my admiration, which is the level of support I feel I'd need. Unfortunately - the books that fall flat are always a little easier to talk about....<br /><br /><br />I'd rather be disappointed with myself than with anything Zadie-related, but after reading a bunch of GR reviews, I had to admit that I'm among many readers who found this book disappointing for reasons they were able to cogently identify (and with which I fully agree). (Of course, there are eloquent proponents of the book as well; would that I were among you, my people from whom I've apparently self-exiled!) Since there are already so many other strong reviews that capture what this book tries to do and how it falls flat, I'll limit myself here to itemizing my top five gripes rather than launching into a full-on takedown. Perhaps this will also alleviate my guilt at betraying Zadie.<br /><br /><br />1. When word spread that Zadie had a new book coming, there was much rejoicing - and then an additional wave of rejoicing that this book would be about dancing. Through GR, I know I'm among many readers who love a good dance-related book; perhaps we are all still mourning the premature demise of Bunheads (anyone?). But, for a book in which dance supposedly functions as a central metaphor, Swing Time seems frightfully deficient in notable literary or philosophical writing about dancing. This is an especially egregious error in that Zadie Smith is certainly capable of doing the most elegant, affecting, poetic, and deft things with language. And wow, it seems like a passionate and visceral subject such as The Dance would have been an amazing target for her dazzling linguistics and insights! Sure, there's dancing going on IN the book: it's a recurring plot device in both the "Tracey/London" and "Aimee/Africa" sections, and as indicated by the book's title, the narrator references significant moments in the history of dance and their racial and cultural implications. But this is all as dispassionate and dry as the term paper it sounds like. There are no passionate descriptions, or even especially descriptive descriptions, of actual dance. There is much talk of the importance of dance and the love of dance, but absolutely no showing of it. A YA potboiler about vampire ballerinas would probably contain more fascinating writing about actual dance. The power of dance as conveyed through this book would be comparable to reading Billie Holiday's biography - a sad story, to be sure - but without ever actually hearing her songs or voice to bring that story to life.<br /><br />2. You know how everyone is annoyed by Nick Carraway, especially when played in a film by Tobey McGuire, since in my experience everyone also seems annoyed by Tobey McGuire?...no, just me and the folks I know? Anyway, my point is that a passively observant (but not especially insightful or perceptive), shallowly characterized and non-evolving cipher of a narrator can be hard to love, and in Swing Time we have an excellent specimen. The narrator is unnamed, leading myself and others to consider the possibility that this character is "meant to be this (bland) way" in service of some grander big picture literary project. Maybe, but if you have to consider granting a special concession to the author on this kind of basis, then that literary project just didn't work.<br /><br />3. Oh, and even if you don't mind the narrator, never fear: there are many other under- or un-developed characters to choose from. Specifically, most of the characters in Africa, Aimee and her entourage, and quite honestly, even Tracey (especially post-childhood). With most of these characters, I didn't get a solid sense of how they talked, looked, or behaved, what they believed, or what motivated their actions, which often seemed incongruous and came out of nowhere. In some of Zadie's other books, we get vivid stream-of-consciousness internal monologues from a diversity of characters, which worked a hell of a lot better for me than having everything filtered through the limited no-name-ator of this book. As others have observed, the narrator's mother is by far the most developed, multi-dimensional, interesting character, and I enjoyed parts of this book as a kind of novella about her. <br /><br /><br />4. The character of Aimee warrants a special critique. If, of all possible jobs that exist, you decide as an author to make your narrator/protagonist the personal assistant of a huge international pop star, then I feel like you have a responsibility to do more with the international pop star character than merely use her as a plot mechanism to send the narrator on periodic visits to Africa. Surely there are other ways to accomplish this, or even to incorporate the themes the Africa sections attempt to address. Aimee appears in only a few short scenes in the book, and her instant transformation into a clueless would-be humanitarian isn't discussed or described but rather a thing we are simply meant to understand and accept - again, lots of telling with no showing. Aimee is so shallowly characterized that getting any idea of what she is like relies on the reader's own ability to collage together attributes of various known celebrities (something I wasn't really able to do because I just don't give a shit about them). I know Zadie Smith is a hardworking author, so I was confused because this technique just seems super lazy and pointless. Also, I was resentful that I was forced to try to conjure up thoughts of people like Angelina Jolie when a big reason I read in the first place is to try to get away from pervasive celebrity culture that I don't give a crap about.<br /><br /><br />5. There are authors people read for plot, and Zadie Smith isn't one of them and doesn't need to be. So I was dismayed at how this book ultimately resorts to cumbersome plot devices, which toward the conclusion especially become dramatic and contrived. Without giving spoilers (and btw: having to worry about spoilers in a Zadie Smith book? Damn, that's cray-cray!), more stuff happens in the final chunk of pages than in large preceding swaths of the book. Even the whole shuttling back and forth in alternate chapters between London and Africa, past and present, and trying to tie together various loose ends, all feels belabored and forced. Like many readers, I came to dread the non-London chapters because their execution seemed so inferior in comparison.<br /><br /><br />What makes me saddest overall is that I really liked this book in its earliest pages, where Zadie uses her characteristic portrayal of diverse working-class London girlhood to begin exploring the themes and big ideas with which this book is concerned. I honestly think she could have continued on in this vein and wound up with a more effective and powerful book in the tradition of NW and White Teeth. I respect the author's right to embark on a new kind of project, but unfortunately, this book, and the reading of it, felt exactly like a project in the end.</span>
  <a data-text-id="59526067599381957" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_1884896553" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1884896553" />
    <a title="James" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/41609617-james"><img alt="James" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1539156508p2/41609617.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1884896553?book_show_action=true">Jan 26, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="James" class="user" itemprop="url" name="James" href="/user/show/41609617-james">James</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781594203985" href="/book/show/29734992-swing-time">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1884896553" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4011533989959098200">This is a great novel and I think one of the strongest (if not the strongest) of Zadie Smith’s already impressive body of work.<br/><br/>The story works on many levels and takes in multifarious themes, which although are generally familiar territory for Zadie Smith, are approached in what feels like a very focussed, new and intelligent way.<br/><br/>There is so much in this novel, it is difficult to know where to start, challenging to encapsulate – but in an attempt to try and convey… This is a story of friendship,</span>
  <span id="freeText4011533989959098200" style="display:none">This is a great novel and I think one of the strongest (if not the strongest) of Zadie Smith’s already impressive body of work.<br /><br />The story works on many levels and takes in multifarious themes, which although are generally familiar territory for Zadie Smith, are approached in what feels like a very focussed, new and intelligent way.<br /><br />There is so much in this novel, it is difficult to know where to start, challenging to encapsulate – but in an attempt to try and convey… This is a story of friendship, family, betrayal, ambition, life and death, race and racial politics, power, class and gender; but it is much more than that – questions are suggested concerning – what is success, what is privilege – what is it and what does it actually mean to any of us? <br /><br />The story moves from North London, to the USA to Africa and raises questions about belonging….not just geographical or racial belonging, but family belonging, friendship and community belonging…<br /><br />The story tracks through the 1980’s and 90’s and whilst very evocative (particularly of London) successfully avoids the usual clichés of lazy nostalgia. <br /><br />Concerning the power of celebrity, think Madonna, Shakira, Angelina Jolie, Oprah Winfrey et al. But this is no polemic or diatribe – instead as with all great books, questions are raised, thoughts are provoked and judgements are not made.<br /><br />You very much feel as though you have been on a journey – I think that’s possibly the best way to describe this novel. It’s a very satisfying, compelling, thought provoking, challenging, emotionally engaging and memorable journey – of great reality, humanity, meaning and authenticity. <br /> <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4011533989959098200" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1837670237" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1837670237" />
    <a title="Monica" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4739465-monica"><img alt="Monica" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1577896647p2/4739465.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1837670237?book_show_action=true">Jan 31, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Monica" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Monica" href="/user/show/4739465-monica">Monica</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4739465-monica?shelf=worldwide">worldwide</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4739465-monica?shelf=pub_2010s">pub_2010s</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4739465-monica?shelf=female-author-fic">female-author-fic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4739465-monica?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4739465-monica?shelf=women-savvy">women-savvy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4739465-monica?shelf=aoc-female">aoc-female</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4739465-monica?shelf=racial-matters">racial-matters</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4739465-monica?shelf=arc-grgiveaway-free">arc-grgiveaway-free</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1837670237" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3327734353601372291">Great book! I won this in a goodreads giveaway. This was my first Zadie Smith novel. It won't be my last. There is more to this Swing Time than it seems. The novel is ostensibly about two girls growing up in London with dreams of dancing. A coming of age story. But it's much more than that. This book is a thoughtful discussion of isms and cultural disconnects. Smith's writings cumulatively addressed racism, sexism, feminism, multiculturalism, classism, socialism, colonialism, altruism, exoticism</span>
  <span id="freeText3327734353601372291" style="display:none">Great book! I won this in a goodreads giveaway. This was my first Zadie Smith novel. It won't be my last. There is more to this Swing Time than it seems. The novel is ostensibly about two girls growing up in London with dreams of dancing. A coming of age story. But it's much more than that. This book is a thoughtful discussion of isms and cultural disconnects. Smith's writings cumulatively addressed racism, sexism, feminism, multiculturalism, classism, socialism, colonialism, altruism, exoticism, and even fetishism. But it doesn't stop there, the book also addresses issues of cultural identity, the importance of education, parenting, and a scathing indictment of a lack of cultural awareness and personal growth through the evolution (or lack thereof) and squandered opportunities in the character of the narrator.<br /><br />Smith writes the book in the first person so the reader views the world through the eyes of the nondescript narrator. The way that Smith does this is masterful because it encourages the reader to drape their own perceptions and judgments onto the character. People seem to view things and circumstances differently because the narrator is so insubstantial. I think that is an interesting writing perspective and difficult to do well. It is through the secondary characters and the narrator's perceptions that put meat on the bones of the narrator so to speak.<br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[The story is of two young girls both bi-racial and both lower middle class (Tracey and the narrator). Even the form of bi-racial existence seems to have meaning. Tracey has a white mother while the narrator has a white father. There seems to be an implicit assertion of stability here. Not sure of the intent. Smith seems to imply that the blackness introduces some <s>instability</s> restlessness into the home. In Tracey's life her mother is ever present and her father is unreliable and mercurial sometimes violent. Because of his inconsistency he is also exotic and exciting to Tracey. Tracey's mother is uneducated and unambitious. As a single parent, she is overwhelmed with trying to build a life for her daughter. She is supportive of her daughter by cultivating her dancing abilities which she believes is hereditary. In contrast the narrator's mother is Jamaican. Coming from humble beginnings, she is restless and driven towards a better life. She believes that is through education and spends the novel bettering herself and ensuring that her daughter has the similar opportunities. The narrator's father in contrast is present, steady, dependable, patient and supportive. He like Tracey's mother, seems to be irresistibly attracted to the exoticism and restlessness of the "other". Both embracing a fantasy to the detriment of their own lives. As the girls grow, they start to diverge and the differences and impacts of their upbringing becomes more apparent.<br /><br />The middle deals with the narrator experiences with Aimee (a popstar ala Madonna. The narrator becomes one of her personal assistants) with periodic flashbacks to childhood to highlight the narrators perspective. What struck me about the Aimee portions of the book was the shift in messaging. The point of view of the narrator becomes an avatar for first world order; the embodiment of how Great Britain (and the USA) see themselves on the world stage. Aimee is aging rich and fit and full of energy. Aimee is a force of nature oblivious to her cultural footprint. She decides to fund an all-girls school in a small village in Africa. This seemingly benevolent act creates much more chaos and barriers for the village than help but Aimee doesn't see a downside. Mostly because she doesn't want to and she is rich enough to create that reality in sphere of influence. She even starts a fantasy love affair with an African (Lamin) who is much younger. The affair is symbolic of colonialism In return Aimee exploits the African rhythms and drums which she incorporated into her album. But wealth and prosperity are measured differently in different cultures. Eerily reminiscent of the Indians being offered trinkets and TB infected blankets in exchange for food to keep the colonists alive. The reality in the African village proved quite different. The school created divisiveness within the village. The boys came to resent it because their needs were not addressed. Since the village was getting money from Aimee; the government stopped providing government services to the village putting the resources elsewhere, thereby overall disadvantaging the village. Just like the first world, oblivious to unintended consequences. Then once they are made aware, they shrug their shoulders and try and save a few. On to the next. <br /><br />Tracey ends up being a bitter, unemployed single mother. She seems to believe that the narrator's mother owes her. It was unclear why she felt that way. It was also unclear why the narrator's mother felt such an obligation to Tracey, in the end telling the narrator that she should raise Tracey's kids. It's almost like she saw much of herself in Tracey (the smart, restless, exuberant), but the narrator was more of the person she strived to be (smart, capable, accomplished). Obviously there is a lot more to unpack there. Interestingly enough I also got a subtle bit of misandry in Smith's characterizations. The males in the book do not come across very well. For example when Tracey was asked about why she couldn't continue working after her mother died. Where were their fathers? Tracey replied: <blockquote>"Well, as you can see, I tried vanilla, café au lait and chocolate and you know what I figured out? On the inside, they're all the fucking same: men."</blockquote><br />Not even the narrator's father though rock steady with his daughter, he had questionable priorities with 2 kids from a previous marriage that he never saw and the rather fetishistic (though potentially false) accusation from Tracey. Even though none of the women are that nice either, they are better characterized making their flaws more comprehensible.<br /><br />Meanwhile the narrator is exposed to what should be life altering experiences. She meets really deep and thoughtful people and travels all over the world. She helps to run a multimillion dollar business and yet at the end of the novel shows absolutely no personal growth. Doesn't see the world through different eyes. This for me was symbolic of cultural indifference and a huge lack of social (self) awareness. In the end narrator comes across as emotionless and completely oblivious to her privilege. This lack of emotional substance seems to be Smith's way of enabling readers to impress their own reactions upon the character to fill the void. Doggone it, prior to reading this novel, I was pretty confident that I liked me, but the narrator comes off as shallow and superficial (much like my perception of American and British foreign policies).  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />On the whole a very deep and profound novel with nuggets of depth that sneaked into the most unexpected places. I really enjoyed it! If anything, Smith packed almost too much symbolism into this story. <br /><br /><i>vacillating between 4 and 5 Stars</i><br /><br /><b>Edited to Add:</b> Read the (dead tree) hardcover edition.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3327734353601372291" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1750097902" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1750097902" />
    <a title="Sam" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/59357213-sam"><img alt="Sam" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1478880459p2/59357213.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1750097902?book_show_action=true">Nov 03, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sam" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sam" href="/user/show/59357213-sam">Sam</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781594203985" href="/book/show/29734992-swing-time">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/59357213-sam?shelf=2016-reads">2016-reads</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1750097902" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15588557981156334789"><i>
  <b>"I saw all my years at once, but they were not piled up on each other, experience after experience, building into something of substance - the opposite. A truth was being revealed to me: that I had always tried to attach myself to the light of other people, that I never had any light of my own. I experienced myself as a kind of shadow."</b>
</i><br/><br/>This quote from Zadie Smith's <i>Swing Time</i> is very early in the novel, and for me calls out the difficult choices and construction that led me to give the work </span>
  <span id="freeText15588557981156334789" style="display:none"><i>
  <b>"I saw all my years at once, but they were not piled up on each other, experience after experience, building into something of substance - the opposite. A truth was being revealed to me: that I had always tried to attach myself to the light of other people, that I never had any light of my own. I experienced myself as a kind of shadow."</b>
</i><br /><br />This quote from Zadie Smith's <i>Swing Time</i> is very early in the novel, and for me calls out the difficult choices and construction that led me to give the work a <b>3.5 star</b> review, with a round down to <b>3 stars</b>. Her prose is excellent, some of her characters very compelling, and she finds creative ways to explore differences and nuances and make observations on race, class, education, and worldview, with her cast of characters interacting and colliding, speaking and not speaking to reveal these ideas and truths. But our guide to this world and this story, the narrator, is very flat, boring, and from child to adult is more or less the same, experiencing little personal growth. And the jumping around through time, instead of following in a linear fashion the narrator's experience, seems like a construction for its own sake but it hinders rather than helps the narrative.<br /><br />Let's start with what works: the writing itself is beautiful. Mostly soft, slow, calming prose, but with deft observations and little twists of phrase to keep you going. Her grasp of the power complexities of relationships is great, and she shows us the various ways these power imbalances express themselves in many of the key relationships between the characters: narrator/Tracey, Aimee/everyone, narrator/mother, narrator mother/narrator father, and the list goes on. The narrator's mother and Tracey in particular are very well drawn, compelling, forces of nature that captivate and hold the narrator's attention (and ours). And the representation of race and class and education level across the board is extremely varied and presented with some sharp, smart ideas, revealed by the narrator and other characters mainly via their interactions rather than direct quotes. Smith is clearly a talented writer.<br /><br />And now what didn't work as well for me: the narrator. That first quote really encapsulates my issues with her, she is far less compelling and memorable than many of the other characters she interacts with, and her journey from childhood to adulthood, with all the trials and tribulations she and family and friends endure, doesn't result in any real character growth or awareness or anything. She's a shadow, a cipher, with flashes of real feeling and personality but too often a cardboard cutout to observe into other characters' lives. And maybe that was Smith's point in making her the narrator, but it can lead to a somewhat stifled, irritating read.<br /><br />The other main issue was the time jumping, and divide of experiences between childhood/focus on Tracey and adulthood/focus on Aimee. Tracey and Aimee are the two bodies the narrator orbits around, with characters like her formidable mother bridging the gap. With Tracey, we get school and much of the dance and early family dynamics; with Aimee it's adult relationship turmoil and celebrity culture and white (and mixed) do-gooders in Africa; for both Smith's commentary on race and class follow. The main problem is that the Tracey parts are far more interesting than the Aimee parts on the whole. Tracey herself is magnetic: spoiled, broken, talented, cruel, petty... just as the narrator can't look away from Tracey when she is on stage dancing, it's hard to tear away from Tracey when she's present on the page. And the accompanying observations and distinctions made, between Tracey's mother and narrator's mother, the role of fathers (one white, one black), the class and education intersections, are light, smart, fascinating. By contrast, some of the Aimee parts are plain boring. There's interesting commentary and observations, sure, when they are setting up the school. But you're never as in tune with the personalities and interactions of Hawa, Aimee, Fern, and Lamin as you are with the narrator's family and Tracey's family. And some of the observations lack as subtle a touch when it comes to undermining perceived know-better and privilege. And so, find myself interested in/caring for half the characters, and somewhat bored by the narrator, it could make some chapters feel very slow and I got bogged down more easily than I thought I would, though others would fly by.<br /><br />I suppose I went into this book expecting more, and was mildly disappointed. It's my first Zadie Smith novel, and I will pick up others down the line since I did like her writing and it's entirely possible another story will call to me. But for me, the best books, the ones I give 4 and 5 stars to, they move the head and the heart, resonate intellectually and emotionally. <i>Swing Time</i> had plenty that worked in terms of the former, but completely missed for me in terms of the latter.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15588557981156334789" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1943094552" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1943094552" />
    <a title="Julie " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5210022-julie"><img alt="Julie " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1356906542p2/5210022.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1943094552?book_show_action=true">Mar 16, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Julie " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Julie " href="/user/show/5210022-julie">Julie </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5210022-julie?shelf=2017">2017</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5210022-julie?shelf=e-book">e-book</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5210022-julie?shelf=overdrive">overdrive</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5210022-julie?shelf=contemporary-fiction">contemporary-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5210022-julie?shelf=fiction-literature">fiction-literature</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1943094552" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer895888565510851575">Swing Time by Zadie Smith is a 2016 Hamish Hamilton publication. <br/><br/>I picked this book out while reading through some of 2016’s award nominees, eager to read outside my comfort zone for a while. <br/><br/> The story begins with the childhood friendship between two ‘brown girls’ who forge a strong bond, despite their difference in upbringing and approach to life. <br/><br/>Tracey is a talented dancer, while her unnamed friend dances to the beat of a different drummer, so to speak. <br/><br/>As we follow the paths these two embar</span>
  <span id="freeText895888565510851575" style="display:none">Swing Time by Zadie Smith is a 2016 Hamish Hamilton publication. <br /><br />I picked this book out while reading through some of 2016’s award nominees, eager to read outside my comfort zone for a while. <br /><br /> The story begins with the childhood friendship between two ‘brown girls’ who forge a strong bond, despite their difference in upbringing and approach to life. <br /><br />Tracey is a talented dancer, while her unnamed friend dances to the beat of a different drummer, so to speak. <br /><br />As we follow the paths these two embark on in life, the friendship that once cemented them together, begins to crack. Still, no matter what transpires for each of them, that bond seems indestructible and impossible to ignore completely. <br /><br />This book is truly multi-layered. Tracey has a goal, a purpose, despite her troubled home life. But, her undisciplined childhood promotes unflattering behaviors in her. <br /><br />Our narrator, may not possess the natural talent of her friend, but her life is grounded by a father who loves her and a strong mother who works hard to achieve her own goals, set an example for her daughter, and hopefully provide her with a better life in the process. <br /><br />The story is filled with lush locations, introduces us to different cultures, and even has musical soundtrack. The writing, of course, is exceptional, and characterizations vivid and alive. <br /><br />The women featured are so exotic, each with a distinct and profound sense of themselves, except perhaps for our narrator who is still on that path of self -discovery. <br /><br />The pop star the narrator works for might conjure up an image or two of real life divas, the mother is a character who leaves a strong and forceful impression on her daughter, and there is no question that Tracey’s friendship has had an impact. Hawa is a unique character, as well. A woman who embraces her life despite the cultural judgements our sensibilities might balk at. All these women make an impression on the narrator in some way and will help her chose her path. <br /><br />I hope I’ve taken from the book what the author was attempting to express, but for some reason, I find it difficult to articulate what that might be. <br /><br /> I think our storyteller was so focused on leaving her old neighborhood behind, once she manages to do that, she seems to struggle with her selfhood. She attempts to wear several hats, but they don’t always seem to fit. <br /><br />“I saw all my years at once, but they were not piled up on each other, experience after experience, building into something of substance—the opposite. A truth was being revealed to me: that I had always tried to attach myself to the light of other people, that I had never had any light of my own. I experienced myself as a kind of shadow.”<br /><br />Ultimately, I think our narrator will find her calling, her niche, will embrace her heritage, her roots, and make peace with who she is deep down and maybe even find a common ground with her old childhood friend, in the old neighborhood where they first danced. <br /><br />Overall, this is a well written story, which I think is open to various interpretations. It’s a serious character analysis, it’s about personal convictions, friendship, race, and individuality. This is my first novel by this author, and I’m impressed enough, I want to have a look at her previous releases and will look forward enjoying her future efforts. <br />4 stars<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="895888565510851575" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1562189962" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1562189962" />
    <a title="Rebecca" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5875398-rebecca"><img alt="Rebecca" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1599578317p2/5875398.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1562189962?book_show_action=true">Oct 13, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rebecca" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rebecca" href="/user/show/5875398-rebecca">Rebecca</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/29424969-swing-time">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5875398-rebecca?shelf=reviewed-bookbrowse">reviewed-bookbrowse</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5875398-rebecca?shelf=read-via-netgalley">read-via-netgalley</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5875398-rebecca?shelf=booker-longlisted">booker-longlisted</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1562189962" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12335988304457171539">Smith’s fifth novel spans 25 years and journeys from London to New York City and West Africa in tracing the different paths two black girls’ lives take. The narrator (who is never named) and Tracey, both biracial, meet through dance lessons at age seven in 1982 and soon become inseparable. The way this relationship shifts over time is the most potent element of the novel, and will appeal to fans of Elena Ferrante. The narrator alternates chapters about her friendship with Tracey with chapters ab</span>
  <span id="freeText12335988304457171539" style="display:none">Smith’s fifth novel spans 25 years and journeys from London to New York City and West Africa in tracing the different paths two black girls’ lives take. The narrator (who is never named) and Tracey, both biracial, meet through dance lessons at age seven in 1982 and soon become inseparable. The way this relationship shifts over time is the most potent element of the novel, and will appeal to fans of Elena Ferrante. The narrator alternates chapters about her friendship with Tracey with chapters about her work for pop star Aimee in Africa. Unfortunately, the Africa material is not very convincing or lively and I was impatient for these sections to finish. The Aimee subplot and the way Tracey turns out struck me as equally clichéd. Despite the geographical and chronological sprawl, the claustrophobic narration makes this feel insular, defusing its potential messages about how race, money and class still define and divide us. A new Zadie Smith novel is an event; this one is still worth reading, but it definitely disappointed me in comparison to <i>White Teeth</i> and <i>On Beauty</i>.</span>

          </span>

<div id="review_1833473288" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="Jenny (Reading Envy)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/68030-jenny-reading-envy"><img alt="Jenny (Reading Envy)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1455391853p2/68030.jpg" /></a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jenny (Reading Envy)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jenny (Reading Envy)" href="/user/show/68030-jenny-reading-envy">Jenny (Reading Envy)</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68030-jenny-reading-envy?shelf=booker-winners-and-listed">booker-winners-and-listed</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1833473288" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8318540972426577755">This book took me a while to read because I took breaks from it to read other things. The fact that I found it so easy to put down is probably not a good sign, and I kept coming back to it because everyone kept saying how good it was. It ended up on a lot of year-end best-of lists. Zadie Smith herself is well-respected and an excellent speaker and purveyor of all things feminist. I kept feeling like I was supposed to like this novel more than I actually did.<br/><br/>Part of it was the storytelling techni</span>
  <span id="freeText8318540972426577755" style="display:none">This book took me a while to read because I took breaks from it to read other things. The fact that I found it so easy to put down is probably not a good sign, and I kept coming back to it because everyone kept saying how good it was. It ended up on a lot of year-end best-of lists. Zadie Smith herself is well-respected and an excellent speaker and purveyor of all things feminist. I kept feeling like I was supposed to like this novel more than I actually did.<br /><br />Part of it was the storytelling technique, I think. The story of the two girls who grow up into different lives on the outskirts of London was interesting, but the story is told at arms length. The emotions and inner workings are never on display until they come to the surface in an emotional outburst. I never felt connected to the two women. Then the central character ends up working for an aging pop star and her charity work in Africa, and somehow the lessons I felt we were supposed to "learn" there didn't have the punch they could or should have. <br /><br />It is a novel about race and privilege and how that varies from place to place, also about how strong our parents are effects us whether or not we notice it while we grow. But just the same, I find it somehow forgettable.<br /><br />It probably didn't help that I was also reading <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17465515.The_Story_of_a_New_Name__The_Neapolitan_Novels__2_" title="The Story of a New Name (The Neapolitan Novels #2) by Elena Ferrante" rel="nofollow noopener">The Story of a New Name</a> at the same time, also about two girls who were friends and grew into womanhood with different amounts of privilege. Only in that case, the emotional complexity changes everything. I had no empathy with the characters in Swing Time. <br /><br /><i>Thanks to the publisher for providing a review copy through NetGalley.</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="8318540972426577755" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2049994807" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2049994807" />
    <a title="PattyMacDotComma" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1128612-pattymacdotcomma"><img alt="PattyMacDotComma" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1577835191p2/1128612.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2049994807?book_show_action=true">Jan 29, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="PattyMacDotComma" class="user" itemprop="url" name="PattyMacDotComma" href="/user/show/1128612-pattymacdotcomma">PattyMacDotComma</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1128612-pattymacdotcomma?shelf=aa">aa</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1128612-pattymacdotcomma?shelf=fiction-adult">fiction-adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1128612-pattymacdotcomma?shelf=aa-ce">aa-ce</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1128612-pattymacdotcomma?shelf=award-win-listed">award-win-listed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1128612-pattymacdotcomma?shelf=kindle">kindle</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2049994807" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8456808038886890571"><b>3.5★<br/>“That autumn, in my first term at my new school, I found out what I was without my friend: a body without a distinct outline. The kind of girl who moved from group to group, neither welcomed nor despised, tolerated, and always eager to avoid confrontation. I felt I made no impression.”</b><br/><br/>The narrator has no name, and as she tells us her long story, it seems that she has been an unmemorable person, attached sequentially to a lot of self-important people, sometimes being ignored or taken advantag</span>
  <span id="freeText8456808038886890571" style="display:none"><b>3.5★<br />“That autumn, in my first term at my new school, I found out what I was without my friend: a body without a distinct outline. The kind of girl who moved from group to group, neither welcomed nor despised, tolerated, and always eager to avoid confrontation. I felt I made no impression.”</b><br /><br />The narrator has no name, and as she tells us her long story, it seems that she has been an unmemorable person, attached sequentially to a lot of self-important people, sometimes being ignored or taken advantage of and sometimes basking in their reflected glory. I didn’t care for any of the people, but Smith certainly does have a way with words.<br /><br />This was longlisted for the 2017 Booker Prize and won other awards. I’m not sure why. I felt as if I were being hit over the head with explanations of why people were behaving the way they were rather than just reading a good story. <br /><br />It covers a lot of territory. She and her childhood friend Tracey grew up near each other in London. They each had one white parent and one black parent.<br /><br /><b>“My mother was a feminist. She wore her hair in a half-inch Afro, her skull was perfectly shaped, she never wore make-up and dressed us both as plainly as possible. Hair is not essential when you look like Nefertiti.<br />. . . <br />her mother was white, obese, afflicted with acne. She wore her thin blond hair pulled back very tightly in what I knew my mother would call a ‘Kilburn facelift’.”</b><br /> <br />Her father was a kindly postal worker and Tracey’s father was a con-artist who was doing time in jail when they met. Four extremely different people, and yet<br /><br /><b>“Our shade of brown was exactly the same – as if one piece of tan material had been cut to make us both – and our freckles gathered in the same areas, we were of the same height.”</b><br /><br />They met on their way to dance class and both became obsessed with Fred Astaire, musicals, and movie stars. Tracey was the one with the talent and the powerful personality, the one who would stick to the dance, while our narrator attached herself eventually to an Aussie megastar, Aimee, from Bendigo, a regional city in Victoria. <br /><br />Aimee seems to be a Kylie Minogue, with her strong Aussie roots and her loyal sister, but she also has touches of Madonna, with her entourage, her kids, and her fascination with Africa and ease of attracting bed partners. Too much of the story centred on her and her hangers-on and her trips. <br /><br />The narrator’s mother was the one who was responsible for our education (and her own). She filled every possible moment with reading and learning and lecturing her husband and daughter. Her husband found himself further and further removed from her. <br /><br /><b>“My childhood took place in the widening gap. I watched my autodidact mother swiftly, easily, outstrip my father.”</b><br /><br />The mother is almost a caricature of a driven career-woman, although I’m sure she’s not intended to be. She is absolutely determined to do something, better herself, get herself into a position where she can improve things. This is what our narrator grows up with. When she later attaches herself to Aimee, she’s chosen another woman who has the same drive to be the centre of attention, to effect change in the world. What did she really want?<br /><br /><b>“What do we want from our mothers when we are children? Complete submission.<br />. . . <br />all you want from your mother is that she once and for all admit that she is your mother and only your mother, and that her battle with the rest of life is over. She has to lay down arms and come to you.”</b><br /><br />Of course, she’s never going to get that, but she does have a best friend. The two brown girls are inseparable when they’re very young, although they are quite different people. As they grow up, they cross paths every now and then. I imagine many people have had this feeling but not been able to put it into words quite so well.<br /><br /><b>“I felt like some old diary she’d found in a drawer: a reminder of a more innocent and foolish time in her life.”</b><br /><br />The timelines move back and forth and the action moves from her simple English childhood to an African village to the bright lights of New York. But I never felt she fit anywhere, and I never really had a sense of who she is. I'm not sure she did either.<br /><br />This is when I really wish for half-stars, because I can't decide if I am so disappointed it's only 3, or the writing is good enough to boost it to 4. At the moment, I am disappointed, so 3 it is.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8456808038886890571" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2060999289" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2060999289" />
    <a title="Marie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/51069187-marie"><img alt="Marie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1453474042p2/51069187.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2060999289?book_show_action=true">Sep 17, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Marie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Marie" href="/user/show/51069187-marie">Marie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/51069187-marie?shelf=london">london</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2060999289" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10350226457468841882">A sweeping multi-layered novel that reads like a dance through childhood into adulthood, across cultures, exploring race, class and gender issues. At the heart of this novel is the friendship between two “brown girls” growing up in public housing estates but in school with a largely white community in London. They see each other at dance class and are immediately drawn to each other, to the same tone of skin, similar but opposites. They are opposites in that one has a white obese doting mother t</span>
  <span id="freeText10350226457468841882" style="display:none">A sweeping multi-layered novel that reads like a dance through childhood into adulthood, across cultures, exploring race, class and gender issues. At the heart of this novel is the friendship between two “brown girls” growing up in public housing estates but in school with a largely white community in London. They see each other at dance class and are immediately drawn to each other, to the same tone of skin, similar but opposites. They are opposites in that one has a white obese doting mother that lathers her daughter with praise and attention while the other has a black mother subsumed with leftist politics and educating herself seemingly hardly noticing her daughter. The narrator feels like an accessory to her mother. She feels barely noticed and out of place until her friendship with Tracey begins.<br /><br />The narrator is unnamed throughout the novel and her childhood friend is Tracey, who is boisterous, adventurous, fun loving and narcissistic. The narrator seems to float through the novel on the energy of others. First and foremost, there is Tracey’s energy that dictates their play and social lives. Tracey is a brilliantly talented dancer and though the narrator loves dancing, she lacks Tracey’s talent. They spend countless hours watching videos of Fred Astaire, Ginger Rogers, Michael Jackson to name a few.<br /><br />The narrator’s Jamaican mother, a modern day Nefertiti, is a left wing feminist and activist studying politics and philosophy. The father lacks motivation, but is loving and doting towards his family. This is in sharp contrast to Tracey’s family, where there is an absent father. Tracey creates stories to explain where he is and what he is doing, but it seems he left them and has a new family. Though the narrator’s mother criticizes Tracey’s mother and her habits, the narrator enjoys the quiet of Tracey’s home compared to the anger in her own home where her mother no longer wishes to be married to her father.<br /><br />Jealousies arise and tensions result. The girls in childhood had written stories of “ballet dancers in peril.” Tracey would create and dictate these stories while the narrator transcribed. Always, just as it seemed the happy ending would arrive, disaster would result. Thus, Tracey’s stories foreshadow the end of the beautiful friendship of Tracey and the narrator. Tracey tells the narrator a story about her father, which may be fact or fiction, that causes them to cease speaking to each other for over a decade.<br /><br />The narrator goes off to college and leaves behind Tracey and their friendship. After a few gigs as a dancer, Tracey’s dancing career fades and she is a single mother to three children all by different fathers and is still living in the public housing estates, a fate the narrator’s mother warned against. The narrator begins working for a big name singer/dancer named Aimee. Aimee’s life is large. She has many people who work for her, numerous boyfriends, children by various men, she travels widely, and becomes interested in opening a girls’ school in an un-named country West Africa. The narrator again is living in the shadow of another large personality, not living a life of her own, running on the energy of another. The narrator travels back and forth getting to know the inhabitants this West African country, watching the fall out of diaspora that occurs there as people (especially men) begin to leave.<br /><br />The narrator is eventually drawn back to Tracey through her mother who has been working for Parliament. The narrator’s mother reaches out to the narrator pleading with her to ask Tracey to stop harassing her with countless letters that initially ask for help, but then begin to criticize the government, and her mother, and the inability of anybody to help with her situation. Her mother becomes consumed and tortured by these letters, unable to think of anything else. She is guilt ridden and seemingly identifying Tracey rather than the narrator as her daughter as she is dying,<br /><br />When the narrator confronts Tracey, Tracey asks her who she is trying to be. The narrator’s voice has changed, her life has changed. After leaking the childhood video, Tracey sends it to the narrator with a note saying, “now everyone knows who you really are.” Are we our childhood selves? Is who we are defined by who we connect and interact with? Is that identity forever changing? How much of that identity is tied to gender, class and race? How much of our childhood identity, our moral core, do we keep with us?<br /><br />This novel is beautifully written, incredibly expansive and brings up awesome philosophical questions. There are so many layers to this novel, that one could go on dissecting this for a very long time. I highly recommend this book to everyone. It would make a superb book club book. My one wish for this novel is that the narrator had more presence, but I think that is part of the point of this book. She floats on the energy of others, she is visible in the shadows of her relationship with others. Class, race and gender issues are often seen in reaction to the narrator.<br /><br />For discussion questions, please see: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.book-chatter.com/?p=2040">http://www.book-chatter.com/?p=2040</a>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10350226457468841882" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1819678671" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1819678671" />
    <a title="Helene Jeppesen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/21992464-helene-jeppesen"><img alt="Helene Jeppesen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1528006866p2/21992464.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1819678671?book_show_action=true">Nov 29, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Helene Jeppesen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Helene Jeppesen" href="/user/show/21992464-helene-jeppesen">Helene Jeppesen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1819678671" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer440089620679982770">While reading "Swing Time" - my first novel by Zadie Smith - I felt conflicted. Some parts of it I loved and I could really appreciate the beauty of the characters' story and the writing. Other parts felt dragging to me, and while they were not unimportant they didn't get the attention from me that they maybe needed. <br/>Basically, this is a story of growing up, identity, facing toxic persons as well as learning about yourself and your place in the world. I liked how it deals with so many diverse to</span>
  <span id="freeText440089620679982770" style="display:none">While reading "Swing Time" - my first novel by Zadie Smith - I felt conflicted. Some parts of it I loved and I could really appreciate the beauty of the characters' story and the writing. Other parts felt dragging to me, and while they were not unimportant they didn't get the attention from me that they maybe needed. <br />Basically, this is a story of growing up, identity, facing toxic persons as well as learning about yourself and your place in the world. I liked how it deals with so many diverse topics, but at the same time I felt rootless throughout - where was this going and how was I feeling about it? <br />Our main character goes through an interesting journey, but while I liked reading about her I liked reading about other characters - such as her mother - more. <br />All in all, I definitely see why Zadie Smith is a well-loved author because she definitely has talent. The structure of the plot and the diversity of the themes are strong, there was just something about the novel all in all that made it less appealing to me than what I was hoping for. </span>
  <a data-text-id="440089620679982770" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1875554772" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1875554772" />
    <a title="Jesse" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1432036-jesse"><img alt="Jesse" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1544746042p2/1432036.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1875554772?book_show_action=true">Jan 24, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jesse" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jesse" href="/user/show/1432036-jesse">Jesse</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1432036-jesse?shelf=women-writers">women-writers</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1432036-jesse?shelf=read-in-2017">read-in-2017</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1432036-jesse?shelf=2010-s">2010-s</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1432036-jesse?shelf=authors-of-color">authors-of-color</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1432036-jesse?shelf=contemporary">contemporary</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1875554772" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3411263605935177441">To my mind the allusion of the title—to the <a href='http://www.imdb.com/title/tt0028333/' rel='nofollow noopener'>beloved Astaire/Rogers RKO musical from 1936</a>—is much more than a resonant reference to the protagonist’s lifelong passion for the classic Hollywood movies, but actually signals the overall structure and spirit of the book: this a novel of movement and rhythm and not precision, of giddy expansiveness and not meticulousness. <br/><br/>And as anybody who watches and loves musicals know, imperfection is just part and parcel of the form: that single bummer song in a </span>
  <span id="freeText3411263605935177441" style="display:none">To my mind the allusion of the title—to the <a href="http://www.imdb.com/title/tt0028333/" rel="nofollow noopener">beloved Astaire/Rogers RKO musical from 1936</a>—is much more than a resonant reference to the protagonist’s lifelong passion for the classic Hollywood movies, but actually signals the overall structure and spirit of the book: this a novel of movement and rhythm and not precision, of giddy expansiveness and not meticulousness. <br /><br />And as anybody who watches and loves musicals know, imperfection is just part and parcel of the form: that single bummer song in a score, one routine that just doesn’t <i>quite</i> come off, a narratively jarring pretext for a dance number, retrogressive attitudes and social behaviors that mar technically flawless scenes. Smith’s <i>Swing Time</i> functioned very similarly for me: some of the storylines riveted me, others made me long for more judicious editing. Characters I wanted to get to know better would disappear off of stage right and cede the stage to less compelling ones. But that is kind of the beauty of the entire enterprise: this is a big, messy, sprawling novel full of ideas and subplots and individual moments of sparkling radiance—but sometimes one has to accept some of the rote stuff needed to string them all together. And my god, those moments when everything comes together: just magical. And I found more than enough of them to keep me reading through some 450+ pages.<br /><br />Perhaps I’m coming off too much as an apologist—and I <i>fully</i> admit to being a Zadie Smith fanboy—but I’m also instinctually sympathetic to what I sense is the overall project. Like many great writers before her Smith has opted to hone a more rigorous analytic style for literary and cultural criticism (see her excellent collection <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6425404.Changing_My_Mind_Occasional_Essays" title="Changing My Mind Occasional Essays by Zadie Smith" rel="nofollow noopener">Changing My Mind: Occasional Essays</a>) while fiction continues to serve as a realm for experimentation. No, <i>Swing Time</i> is not a masterpiece, but it feels quite obvious to me that Smith is moving toward one—like the dancers she celebrates through the novel Smith is feeling out prose rhythms, stretching stylistic muscle, undergoing the hard work necessary to scale the dizzying heights of the extraordinary (one need only recall, say, the multi-volume process implied between the raw talent of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/6765.Virginia_Woolf" title="Virginia Woolf" rel="nofollow noopener">Virginia Woolf</a>’s <i>The Voyage Out</i> and the indisputable mastery of <i>Mrs. Dalloway</i> and after; I suspect Smith is on a similar trajectory).<br /><br />And I get it—not everyone particularly wants to see what goes on behind the curtains and between the acts. Fair enough. But I certainly wouldn’t want to miss it myself. <br /><br /><br /><i> “She measured time in pages. Half an hour, to her, meant ten pages read, or fourteen, depending on the size of the type, and when you think of time in this way there isn’t time left for anything else…” </i><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="3411263605935177441" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('QMbP9hCyN1zn0r9eKVla2ZnjLjsdLTLa/TKQKB0/gfABz6ov2S/t7odl6fkcSwD3bWtsQoWaMqaU18mOd4FP1Q==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  }

Lovers at school, the two young women now perform spoken-word poetry together in Dublin, where a journalist named Melissa spots their potential. Drawn into Melissa\'s orbit, Frances is reluctantly impressed by the older woman\'s sophisticated home and tall, handsome husband. Private property, Frances believes, is a cultural evil--and Nick, a bored actor who never quite lived up to his potential, looks like patriarchy made flesh. But however amusing their flirtation seems at first, it gives way to a strange intimacy neither of them expect. As Frances tries to keep her life in check, her relationships increasingly resist her control: with Nick, with her difficult and unhappy father, and finally even with Bobbi. Desperate to reconcile herself to the desires and vulnerabilities of her body, Frances\'s intellectual certainties begin to yield to something new: a painful and disorienting way of living from moment
